</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:General_geometry" title="Template:General geometry"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:General_geometry" title="Template talk:General geometry"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:General_geometry" title="Special:EditPage/Template:General geometry"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Infinitely detailed mathematical structure</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Fractal_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Fractal (disambiguation)">Fractal (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sierpinski-carpet.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f8/Sierpinski-carpet.gif" decoding="async" width="160" height="120" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="120" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Sierpi%C5%84ski_carpet" title="Sierpiński carpet">Sierpiński Carpet</a> - Infinite perimeter and zero area</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mandel_zoom_14_satellite_julia_island.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Mandel_zoom_14_satellite_julia_island.jpg/250px-Mandel_zoom_14_satellite_julia_island.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Mandel_zoom_14_satellite_julia_island.jpg/500px-Mandel_zoom_14_satellite_julia_island.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a><figcaption>Highly magnified area on the boundary of the <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/250px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/330px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/500px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a>: its boundary is a fractal curve with <a href="/wiki/Hausdorff_dimension" title="Hausdorff dimension">Hausdorff dimension</a> 2. (Note that the colored sections of the image are not actually part of the Mandelbrot Set, but rather they are based on how quickly the function that produces it diverges.)</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mandelbrot_12_Encirclements.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Mandelbrot_12_Encirclements.jpg/250px-Mandelbrot_12_Encirclements.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Mandelbrot_12_Encirclements.jpg/500px-Mandelbrot_12_Encirclements.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a><figcaption>Mandelbrot set with 12 encirclements</figcaption></figure>
<p>
<span class="anchor" id="Mandelbrot&#95;zooms"></span>
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mandelbrot_sequence_new.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Mandelbrot_sequence_new.gif/250px-Mandelbrot_sequence_new.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Mandelbrot_sequence_new.gif/320px-Mandelbrot_sequence_new.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Mandelbrot_sequence_new.gif 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="240" /></a><figcaption>Zooming into the boundary of the Mandelbrot set</figcaption></figure>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a <b>fractal</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Shape" title="Shape">geometric shape</a> containing detailed structure at arbitrarily small scales, usually having a <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimension</a> strictly exceeding the <a href="/wiki/Topological_dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="Topological dimension">topological dimension</a>. Many fractals appear similar at various scales, as illustrated in successive magnifications of the <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Falconer&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Falconer-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-patterns&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patterns-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This exhibition of similar patterns at increasingly smaller scales is called <a href="/wiki/Self-similarity" title="Self-similarity">self-similarity</a>, also known as expanding symmetry or unfolding symmetry; if this replication is exactly the same at every scale, as in the <a href="/wiki/Menger_sponge" title="Menger sponge">Menger sponge</a>, the shape is called <a href="/wiki/Affine_geometry" title="Affine geometry">affine</a> self-similar.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gouyet&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gouyet-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fractal geometry relates to the mathematical branch of <a href="/wiki/Measure_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Measure theory">measure theory</a> by their <a href="/wiki/Hausdorff_dimension" title="Hausdorff dimension">Hausdorff dimension</a>.
</p><p>One way that fractals are different from finite <a href="/wiki/Geometric_figures" class="mw-redirect" title="Geometric figures">geometric figures</a> is how they <a href="/wiki/Scaling_(geometry)" title="Scaling (geometry)">scale</a>. Doubling the edge lengths of a filled <a href="/wiki/Polygon" title="Polygon">polygon</a> multiplies its area by four, which is two (the ratio of the new to the old side length) raised to the power of two (the conventional dimension of the filled polygon). Likewise, if the <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> of a filled sphere is doubled, its <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> scales by eight, which is two (the ratio of the new to the old radius) to the power of three (the conventional dimension of the filled sphere). However, if a fractal's one-dimensional lengths are all doubled, the spatial content of the fractal scales by a power that is not necessarily an <a href="/wiki/Integer" title="Integer">integer</a> and is in general greater than its conventional dimension.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This power is called the <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimension</a> of the geometric object, to distinguish it from the conventional dimension (which is formally called the <a href="/wiki/Topological_dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="Topological dimension">topological dimension</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot&#95;Chaos&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot_Chaos-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Analytically, many fractals are nowhere <a href="/wiki/Differentiable_function" title="Differentiable function">differentiable</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An infinite <a href="/wiki/Fractal_curve" title="Fractal curve">fractal curve</a> can be conceived of as winding through space differently from an ordinary line – although it is still <a href="/wiki/Topological_dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="Topological dimension">topologically 1-dimensional</a>, its fractal dimension indicates that it locally fills space more efficiently than an ordinary line.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot&#95;Chaos&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot_Chaos-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sierpinski_carpet_6.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sierpinski_carpet_6.svg/250px-Sierpinski_carpet_6.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sierpinski_carpet_6.svg/330px-Sierpinski_carpet_6.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Sierpinski_carpet_6.svg/500px-Sierpinski_carpet_6.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="900" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_carpet" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierpinski carpet">Sierpinski carpet</a> (to level 6), a fractal with a <a href="/wiki/Topological_dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="Topological dimension">topological dimension</a> of 1 and a <a href="/wiki/Hausdorff_dimension" title="Hausdorff dimension">Hausdorff dimension</a> of 1.893</figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:LineSegment_selfSimilar_svg.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/LineSegment_selfSimilar_svg.svg/250px-LineSegment_selfSimilar_svg.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="54" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/LineSegment_selfSimilar_svg.svg/330px-LineSegment_selfSimilar_svg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/LineSegment_selfSimilar_svg.svg/500px-LineSegment_selfSimilar_svg.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="195" data-file-height="53" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Line_segment" title="Line segment">line segment</a> is <a href="/wiki/Similarity_(geometry)" title="Similarity (geometry)">similar</a> to a proper part of itself, but hardly a fractal.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Starting in the 17th century with notions of <a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">recursion</a>, fractals have moved through increasingly rigorous mathematical treatment to the study of <a href="/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">continuous</a> but not <a href="/wiki/Differentiable_function" title="Differentiable function">differentiable</a> functions in the 19th century by the seminal work of <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Bolzano" title="Bernard Bolzano">Bernard Bolzano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann" title="Bernhard Riemann">Bernhard Riemann</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Weierstrass" title="Karl Weierstrass">Karl Weierstrass</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and on to the coining of the word <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fractal" class="extiw" title="wikt:fractal">fractal</a></i> in the 20th century with a subsequent burgeoning of interest in fractals and computer-based modelling in the 20th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is some disagreement among mathematicians about how the concept of a fractal should be formally defined. Mandelbrot himself summarized it as "beautiful, damn hard, increasingly useful. That's fractals."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More formally, in 1982 Mandelbrot defined <i>fractal</i> as follows: "A fractal is by definition a set for which the <a href="/wiki/Hausdorff_dimension" title="Hausdorff dimension">Hausdorff–Besicovitch dimension</a> strictly exceeds the <a href="/wiki/Topological_dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="Topological dimension">topological dimension</a>."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Later, seeing this as too restrictive, he simplified and expanded the definition to this: "A fractal is a rough or fragmented <a href="/wiki/Shape" title="Shape">geometric shape</a> that can be split into parts, each of which is (at least approximately) a reduced-size copy of the whole."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Still later, Mandelbrot proposed "to use <i>fractal</i> without a pedantic definition, to use <i><a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimension</a></i> as a generic term applicable to <i>all</i> the variants".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The consensus among mathematicians is that theoretical fractals are infinitely self-similar <a href="/wiki/Iteration" title="Iteration">iterated</a> and detailed mathematical constructs, of which many <a href="/wiki/List_of_fractals_by_Hausdorff_dimension" title="List of fractals by Hausdorff dimension">examples</a> have been formulated and studied.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Falconer&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Falconer-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-patterns&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patterns-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fractals are not limited to geometric patterns, but can also describe processes in time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gouyet&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gouyet-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-time&#95;series&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_series-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fractal patterns with various degrees of self-similarity have been rendered or studied in visual, physical, and aural media<sup id="cite&#95;ref-music&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-music-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and found in <a href="#fractals_in_nature">nature</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-heart&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heart-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-cerebellum&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cerebellum-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-neuroscience&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neuroscience-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-branching&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-branching-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="#fractals_in_technology">technology</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-soil&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soil-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-diagnostic&#95;imaging&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diagnostic_imaging-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-medicine&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medicine-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-seismology&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seismology-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="#In_creative_works">art</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-novel&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-novel-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-African&#95;art&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-African_art-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-springer.com&#95;9783319324241&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-springer.com_9783319324241-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fractals are of particular relevance in the field of <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaos theory</a> because they show up in the geometric depictions of most chaotic processes (typically either as attractors or as boundaries between basins of attraction).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The term "fractal" was coined by the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Beno%C3%AEt_Mandelbrot" class="mw-redirect" title="Benoît Mandelbrot">Benoît Mandelbrot</a> in 1975.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mandelbrot based it on the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fractus#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:fractus">frāctus</a></i></span>, meaning "broken" or "fractured", and used it to extend the concept of theoretical fractional <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">dimensions</a> to geometric <a href="/wiki/Patterns_in_nature" title="Patterns in nature">patterns in nature</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot&#95;quote&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot_quote-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Introduction">Introduction</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Introduction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Simple_Fractals.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Simple_Fractals.png/250px-Simple_Fractals.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="100" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Simple_Fractals.png/330px-Simple_Fractals.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Simple_Fractals.png/500px-Simple_Fractals.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="300" /></a><figcaption> A simple fractal tree</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:FractalTree.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/FractalTree.gif/250px-FractalTree.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/FractalTree.gif/500px-FractalTree.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="338" /></a><figcaption>A fractal "tree" to eleven iterations</figcaption></figure>
<p>The word "fractal" often has different connotations for mathematicians and the general public, where the public is more likely to be familiar with <a href="/wiki/Fractal_art" title="Fractal art">fractal art</a> than the mathematical concept. The mathematical concept is difficult to define formally, even for mathematicians, but key features can be understood with a little mathematical background.
</p><p>The feature of "self-similarity", for instance, is easily understood by analogy to zooming in with a lens or other device that zooms in on digital images to uncover finer, previously invisible, new structure. If this is done on fractals, however, no new detail appears; nothing changes and the same pattern repeats over and over, or for some fractals, nearly the same pattern reappears over and over. Self-similarity itself is not necessarily counter-intuitive (e.g., people have pondered self-similarity informally such as in the <a href="/wiki/Infinite_regress" title="Infinite regress">infinite regress</a> in parallel mirrors or the <a href="/wiki/Homunculus" title="Homunculus">homunculus</a>, the little man inside the head of the little man inside the head ...). The difference for fractals is that the pattern reproduced must be detailed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 166, 18">&#58;&#8202;166,&#8202;18&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Falconer&#95;2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Falconer-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot&#95;quote&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot_quote-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This idea of being detailed relates to another feature that can be understood without much mathematical background: Having a <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimension</a> greater than its topological dimension, for instance, refers to how a fractal scales compared to how geometric <a href="/wiki/Shapes" class="mw-redirect" title="Shapes">shapes</a> are usually perceived. A straight line, for instance, is conventionally understood to be one-dimensional; if such a figure is <a href="/wiki/Rep-tile" title="Rep-tile">rep-tiled</a> into pieces each 1/3 the length of the original, then there are always three equal pieces. A solid square is understood to be two-dimensional; if such a figure is rep-tiled into pieces each scaled down by a factor of 1/3 in both dimensions, there are a total of 3<sup>2</sup> = 9 pieces.
</p><p>We see that for ordinary self-similar objects, being n-dimensional means that when it is rep-tiled into pieces each scaled down by a scale-factor of 1/<i>r</i>, there are a total of <i>r</i><sup><i>n</i></sup> pieces. Now, consider the <a href="/wiki/Koch_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Koch curve">Koch curve</a>. It can be rep-tiled into four sub-copies, each scaled down by a scale-factor of 1/3. So, strictly by analogy, we can consider the "dimension" of the Koch curve as being the unique real number <i>D</i> that satisfies 3<sup><i>D</i></sup> = 4. This number is called the <i>fractal dimension</i> of the Koch curve; it is not the conventionally perceived dimension of a curve. In general, a key property of fractals is that the fractal dimension differs from the <i>conventionally understood</i> dimension (formally called the topological dimension).
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:3D_Computer_Generated_Fractal.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/3D_Computer_Generated_Fractal.png/250px-3D_Computer_Generated_Fractal.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/3D_Computer_Generated_Fractal.png/500px-3D_Computer_Generated_Fractal.png 1.5x" data-file-width="7680" data-file-height="5760" /></a><figcaption>3D computer-generated fractal</figcaption></figure>
<p>This also leads to understanding a third feature, that fractals as mathematical equations are "nowhere <a href="/wiki/Differentiable_function" title="Differentiable function">differentiable</a>". In a concrete sense, this means fractals cannot be measured in traditional ways.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gordon&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To elaborate, in trying to find the length of a wavy non-fractal curve, one could find straight segments of some measuring tool small enough to lay end to end over the waves, where the pieces could get small enough to be considered to conform to the curve in the normal manner of <a href="/wiki/Rectifiable_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Rectifiable curve">measuring</a> with a tape measure. But in measuring an infinitely "wiggly" fractal curve such as the Koch snowflake, one would never find a small enough straight segment to conform to the curve, because the jagged pattern would always re-appear, at arbitrarily small scales, essentially pulling a little more of the tape measure into the total length measured each time one attempted to fit it tighter and tighter to the curve. The result is that one must need infinite tape to perfectly cover the entire curve, i.e. the snowflake has an infinite perimeter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="history"></span><span class="anchor" id="koch"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Von_Koch_curve.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Von_Koch_curve.gif/250px-Von_Koch_curve.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="208" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Von_Koch_curve.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="312" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Koch_snowflake" title="Koch snowflake">Koch snowflake</a> is a fractal that begins with an equilateral triangle and then replaces the middle third of every line segment with a pair of line segments that form an equilateral bump.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cantor_set_in_seven_iterations.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Cantor_set_in_seven_iterations.svg/250px-Cantor_set_in_seven_iterations.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Cantor_set_in_seven_iterations.svg/500px-Cantor_set_in_seven_iterations.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="729" data-file-height="118" /></a><figcaption>Cantor (ternary) set</figcaption></figure>
<p>The history of fractals traces a path from chiefly theoretical studies to modern applications in <a href="/wiki/Computer_graphics" title="Computer graphics">computer graphics</a>, with several notable people contributing canonical fractal forms along the way.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
A common theme in traditional <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Africa" title="Architecture of Africa">African architecture</a> is the use of fractal scaling, whereby small parts of the structure tend to look similar to larger parts, such as a circular village made of circular houses.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
According to <a href="/wiki/Clifford_A._Pickover" title="Clifford A. Pickover">Pickover</a>, the mathematics behind fractals began to take shape in the 17th century when the mathematician and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" class="mw-redirect" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> pondered <a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">recursive</a> <a href="/wiki/Self-similarity" title="Self-similarity">self-similarity</a> (although he made the mistake of thinking that only the <a href="/wiki/Straight_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Straight line">straight line</a> was self-similar in this sense).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pickover&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pickover-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In his writings, Leibniz used the term "fractional exponents", but lamented that "Geometry" did not yet know of them.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 405">&#58;&#8202;405&#8202;</span></sup> Indeed, according to various historical accounts, after that point few mathematicians tackled the issues and the work of those who did remained obscured largely because of resistance to such unfamiliar emerging concepts, which were sometimes referred to as mathematical "monsters".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gordon&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus, it was not until two centuries had passed that on July 18, 1872 <a href="/wiki/Karl_Weierstrass" title="Karl Weierstrass">Karl Weierstrass</a> presented the first definition of a <a href="/wiki/Weierstrass_function" title="Weierstrass function">function</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Graph_of_a_function" title="Graph of a function">graph</a> that would today be considered a fractal, having the non-<a href="/wiki/Intuition_(knowledge)" class="mw-redirect" title="Intuition (knowledge)">intuitive</a> property of being everywhere <a href="/wiki/Continuous_function" title="Continuous function">continuous</a> but <a href="/wiki/Nowhere_differentiable" class="mw-redirect" title="Nowhere differentiable">nowhere differentiable</a> at the Royal Prussian Academy of Sciences.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">&#58;&#8202;7&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, the quotient difference becomes arbitrarily large as the summation index increases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Not long after that, in 1883, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Cantor" title="Georg Cantor">Georg Cantor</a>, who attended lectures by Weierstrass,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> published examples of <a href="/wiki/Subset" title="Subset">subsets</a> of the real line known as <a href="/wiki/Cantor_set" title="Cantor set">Cantor sets</a>, which had unusual properties and are now recognized as fractals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 11–24">&#58;&#8202;11–24&#8202;</span></sup> Also in the last part of that century, <a href="/wiki/Felix_Klein" title="Felix Klein">Felix Klein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincaré">Henri Poincaré</a> introduced a category of fractal that has come to be called "self-inverse" fractals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 166">&#58;&#8202;166&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="julia"></span>
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Julia_set_(indigo).png" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Julia_set_%28indigo%29.png/250px-Julia_set_%28indigo%29.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Julia_set_%28indigo%29.png/330px-Julia_set_%28indigo%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Julia_set_%28indigo%29.png/500px-Julia_set_%28indigo%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Julia_set" title="Julia set">Julia set</a>, a fractal related to the Mandelbrot set</figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fractal_tree.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Fractal_tree.gif/250px-Fractal_tree.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Fractal_tree.gif/330px-Fractal_tree.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a9/Fractal_tree.gif 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_gasket" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierpinski gasket">Sierpinski gasket</a> can be generated by a fractal tree.</figcaption></figure>
<p>One of the next milestones came in 1904, when <a href="/wiki/Helge_von_Koch" title="Helge von Koch">Helge von Koch</a>, extending ideas of Poincaré and dissatisfied with Weierstrass's abstract and analytic definition, gave a more geometric definition including hand-drawn images of a similar function, which is now called the <a href="/wiki/Koch_snowflake" title="Koch snowflake">Koch snowflake</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 25">&#58;&#8202;25&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another milestone came a decade later in 1915, when <a href="/wiki/Wac%C5%82aw_Sierpi%C5%84ski" title="Wacław Sierpiński">Wacław Sierpiński</a> constructed his famous <a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_triangle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierpinski triangle">triangle</a> then, one year later, his <a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_carpet" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierpinski carpet">carpet</a>. By 1918, two French mathematicians, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Fatou" title="Pierre Fatou">Pierre Fatou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Julia" title="Gaston Julia">Gaston Julia</a>, though working independently, arrived essentially simultaneously at results describing what is now seen as fractal behaviour associated with mapping <a href="/wiki/Complex_numbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex numbers">complex numbers</a> and iterative functions and leading to further ideas about <a href="/wiki/Strange_attractors" class="mw-redirect" title="Strange attractors">attractors and repellors</a> (i.e., points that attract or repel other points), which have become very important in the study of fractals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Very shortly after that work was submitted, by March 1918, <a href="/wiki/Felix_Hausdorff" title="Felix Hausdorff">Felix Hausdorff</a> expanded the definition of "dimension", significantly for the evolution of the definition of fractals, to allow for sets to have non-integer dimensions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The idea of self-similar curves was taken further by <a href="/wiki/Paul_L%C3%A9vy_(mathematician)" title="Paul Lévy (mathematician)">Paul Lévy</a>, who, in his 1938 paper <i>Plane or Space Curves and Surfaces Consisting of Parts Similar to the Whole</i>, described a new fractal curve, the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9vy_C_curve" title="Lévy C curve">Lévy C curve</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-levy&#95;note&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-levy_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>notes 1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="multifractal"></span><span class="anchor" id="Strange&#95;attractor"></span>
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Karperien_Strange_Attractor_200.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Karperien_Strange_Attractor_200.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Strange_attractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Strange attractor">strange attractor</a> that exhibits <a href="/wiki/Multifractal" class="mw-redirect" title="Multifractal">multifractal</a> scaling</figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Uniform_Triangle_Mass_Center_grade_5_fractal.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Uniform_Triangle_Mass_Center_grade_5_fractal.gif/250px-Uniform_Triangle_Mass_Center_grade_5_fractal.gif" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Uniform_Triangle_Mass_Center_grade_5_fractal.gif/330px-Uniform_Triangle_Mass_Center_grade_5_fractal.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Uniform_Triangle_Mass_Center_grade_5_fractal.gif/500px-Uniform_Triangle_Mass_Center_grade_5_fractal.gif 2x" data-file-width="1150" data-file-height="1150" /></a><figcaption>Uniform mass center triangle fractal</figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:60_degrees_2x_recursive_IFS.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/60_degrees_2x_recursive_IFS.jpg/250px-60_degrees_2x_recursive_IFS.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="158" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/60_degrees_2x_recursive_IFS.jpg/330px-60_degrees_2x_recursive_IFS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/60_degrees_2x_recursive_IFS.jpg/500px-60_degrees_2x_recursive_IFS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="890" data-file-height="704" /></a><figcaption>2x 120 degrees recursive <a href="/wiki/Iterated_function_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Iterated function systems">IFS</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Different researchers have postulated that without the aid of modern computer graphics, early investigators were limited to what they could depict in manual drawings, so lacked the means to visualize the beauty and appreciate some of the implications of many of the patterns they had discovered (the Julia set, for instance, could only be visualized through a few iterations as very simple drawings).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 179">&#58;&#8202;179&#8202;</span></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gordon&#95;30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MacTutor&#95;9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacTutor-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That changed, however, in the 1960s, when <a href="/wiki/Benoit_Mandelbrot" title="Benoit Mandelbrot">Benoit Mandelbrot</a> started writing about self-similarity in papers such as <i><a href="/wiki/How_Long_Is_the_Coast_of_Britain%3F_Statistical_Self-Similarity_and_Fractional_Dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="How Long Is the Coast of Britain? Statistical Self-Similarity and Fractional Dimension">How Long Is the Coast of Britain? Statistical Self-Similarity and Fractional Dimension</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which built on earlier work by <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Fry_Richardson" title="Lewis Fry Richardson">Lewis Fry Richardson</a>.
</p><p>In 1975,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot&#95;quote&#95;28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot_quote-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mandelbrot solidified hundreds of years of thought and mathematical development in coining the word "fractal" and illustrated his mathematical definition with striking computer-constructed visualizations. These images, such as of his canonical <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a>, captured the popular imagination; many of them were based on recursion, leading to the popular meaning of the term "fractal".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gordon&#95;30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-classics&#95;8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-classics-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pickover&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pickover-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1980, <a href="/wiki/Loren_Carpenter" title="Loren Carpenter">Loren Carpenter</a> gave a presentation at the <a href="/wiki/SIGGRAPH" title="SIGGRAPH">SIGGRAPH</a> where he introduced his software for generating and rendering fractally generated landscapes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="characteristics"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Definition_and_characteristics">Definition and characteristics</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Definition and characteristics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One often cited description that Mandelbrot published to describe geometric fractals is "a rough or fragmented <a href="/wiki/Shape" title="Shape">geometric shape</a> that can be split into parts, each of which is (at least approximately) a reduced-size copy of the whole";<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> this is generally helpful but limited. Authors disagree on the exact definition of <i>fractal</i>, but most usually elaborate on the basic ideas of self-similarity and the unusual relationship fractals have with the space they are embedded in.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gouyet&#95;5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gouyet-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Falconer&#95;2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Falconer-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One point agreed on is that fractal patterns are characterized by <a href="/wiki/Fractal_dimension" title="Fractal dimension">fractal dimensions</a>, but whereas these numbers quantify <a href="/wiki/Complexity" title="Complexity">complexity</a> (i.e., changing detail with changing scale), they neither uniquely describe nor specify details of how to construct particular fractal patterns.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1975 when Mandelbrot coined the word "fractal", he did so to denote an object whose <a href="/wiki/Hausdorff%E2%80%93Besicovitch_dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="Hausdorff–Besicovitch dimension">Hausdorff–Besicovitch dimension</a> is greater than its <a href="/wiki/Lebesgue_covering_dimension" title="Lebesgue covering dimension">topological dimension</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot&#95;quote&#95;28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot_quote-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, this requirement is not met by <a href="/wiki/Space-filling_curve" title="Space-filling curve">space-filling curves</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Hilbert_curve" title="Hilbert curve">Hilbert curve</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-space&#95;filling&#95;note&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-space_filling_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>notes 2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the trouble involved in finding one definition for fractals, some argue that fractals should not be strictly defined at all. According to <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Falconer_(mathematician)" title="Kenneth Falconer (mathematician)">Falconer</a>, fractals should be only generally characterized by a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gestalt" class="extiw" title="wikt:gestalt">gestalt</a> of the following features;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Falconer&#95;2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Falconer-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Self-similarity, which may include:</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>Exact self-similarity: identical at all scales, such as the <a href="#koch">Koch snowflake</a></li>
<li>Quasi self-similarity: approximates the same pattern at different scales; may contain small copies of the entire fractal in distorted and degenerate forms; e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a>'s satellites are approximations of the entire set, but not exact copies.</li>
<li>Statistical self-similarity: repeats a pattern <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastically</a> so numerical or statistical measures are preserved across scales; e.g., <a href="#random">randomly generated fractals</a> like the well-known example of the <a href="/wiki/How_Long_Is_the_Coast_of_Britain%3F_Statistical_Self-Similarity_and_Fractional_Dimension" class="mw-redirect" title="How Long Is the Coast of Britain? Statistical Self-Similarity and Fractional Dimension">coastline of Britain</a> for which one would not expect to find a segment scaled and repeated as neatly as the repeated unit that defines fractals like the Koch snowflake.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Qualitative self-similarity: as in a time series<sup id="cite&#95;ref-time&#95;series&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_series-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Multifractal" class="mw-redirect" title="Multifractal">Multifractal</a> scaling: characterized by more than one fractal dimension or scaling rule</li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Fine or detailed structure at arbitrarily small scales. A consequence of this structure is fractals may have <a href="/wiki/Emergent_properties" class="mw-redirect" title="Emergent properties">emergent properties</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (related to the next criterion in this list).</li>
<li>Irregularity locally and globally that cannot easily be described in the language of traditional <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a> other than as the limit of a <a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">recursively</a> defined sequence of stages. For images of fractal patterns, this has been expressed by phrases such as "smoothly piling up surfaces" and "swirls upon swirls";<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot&#95;Chaos&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot_Chaos-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><i>see <a href="#algorithms">Common techniques for generating fractals</a></i>.</li></ul>
<p>As a group, these criteria form guidelines for excluding certain cases, such as those that may be self-similar without having other typically fractal features. A straight line, for instance, is self-similar but not fractal because it lacks detail, and is easily described in Euclidean language without a need for recursion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mandelbrot1983&#95;1-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandelbrot1983-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>When Mandelbrot introduced the term fractal, he excluded magnification range as a defining characteristic in order to accommodate physical fractals with more limited ranges than their mathematical counterparts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Common_techniques_for_generating_fractals">Common techniques for generating fractals</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Common techniques for generating fractals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fractal-generating_software" title="Fractal-generating software">Fractal-generating software</a></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="L-system"></span>
</p>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:KarperienFractalBranch.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/KarperienFractalBranch.jpg/250px-KarperienFractalBranch.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="201" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/KarperienFractalBranch.jpg/319px-KarperienFractalBranch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/KarperienFractalBranch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="319" data-file-height="321" /></a><figcaption>Self-similar branching pattern modeled <a href="/wiki/In_silico" title="In silico">in silico</a> using <a href="/wiki/L-systems" class="mw-redirect" title="L-systems">L-systems</a> principles<sup id="cite&#95;ref-branching&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-branching-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p><span class="anchor" id="algorithms"></span>
Images of fractals can be created by <a href="/wiki/Fractal-generating_software" title="Fractal-generating software">fractal generating programs</a>. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Butterfly_effect" title="Butterfly effect">butterfly effect</a>, a small change in a single variable can have an <a href="/wiki/Predictability" title="Predictability">unpredictable</a> outcome.
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Iterated_function_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Iterated function systems">Iterated function systems</a> (IFS)</i> – use fixed geometric replacement rules; may be stochastic or deterministic;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-IFS&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IFS-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> e.g., <a href="/wiki/Koch_snowflake" title="Koch snowflake">Koch snowflake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cantor_set" title="Cantor set">Cantor set</a>, Haferman carpet,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_carpet" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierpinski carpet">Sierpinski carpet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_gasket" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierpinski gasket">Sierpinski gasket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peano_curve" title="Peano curve">Peano curve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dragon_curve" title="Dragon curve">Harter-Heighway dragon curve</a>, <a href="/wiki/T-square_(fractal)" title="T-square (fractal)">T-square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Menger_sponge" title="Menger sponge">Menger sponge</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Strange_attractor" class="mw-redirect" title="Strange attractor">Strange attractors</a></i> – use iterations of a map or solutions of a system of initial-value differential or difference equations that exhibit chaos (e.g., see <a href="#multifractal">multifractal</a> image, or the <a href="/wiki/Logistic_map" title="Logistic map">logistic map</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/L-system" title="L-system">L-systems</a></i> – use string rewriting; may resemble branching patterns, such as in plants, biological cells (e.g., neurons and immune system cells<sup id="cite&#95;ref-branching&#95;18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-branching-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), blood vessels, pulmonary structure,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-modeling&#95;vasculature&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modeling_vasculature-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> etc. or <a href="/wiki/Turtle_graphics" title="Turtle graphics">turtle graphics</a> patterns such as <a href="/wiki/Space-filling_curves" class="mw-redirect" title="Space-filling curves">space-filling curves</a> and tilings</li>
<li><i>Escape-time fractals</i> – use a <a href="/wiki/Formula" title="Formula">formula</a> or <a href="/wiki/Recurrence_relation" title="Recurrence relation">recurrence relation</a> at each point in a space (such as the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a>); usually quasi-self-similar; also known as "orbit" fractals; e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julia_set" title="Julia set">Julia set</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burning_Ship_fractal" title="Burning Ship fractal">Burning Ship fractal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nova_fractal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nova fractal">Nova fractal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyapunov_fractal" title="Lyapunov fractal">Lyapunov fractal</a>. The 2d vector fields that are generated by one or two iterations of escape-time formulae also give rise to a fractal form when points (or pixel data) are passed through this field repeatedly.</li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="random"></span><i>Random fractals</i> – use stochastic rules; e.g., <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9vy_flight" title="Lévy flight">Lévy flight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percolation_theory" title="Percolation theory">percolation clusters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Self-avoiding_walk" title="Self-avoiding walk">self avoiding walks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fractal_landscapes" class="mw-redirect" title="Fractal landscapes">fractal landscapes</a>, trajectories of <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Brownian_tree" title="Brownian tree">Brownian tree</a> (i.e., dendritic fractals generated by modeling <a href="/wiki/Diffusion-limited_aggregation" title="Diffusion-limited aggregation">diffusion-limited aggregation</a> or reaction-limited aggregation clusters).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Finite_subdivision_of_a_radial_link.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Finite_subdivision_of_a_radial_link.png/250px-Finite_subdivision_of_a_radial_link.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="202" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Finite_subdivision_of_a_radial_link.png/330px-Finite_subdivision_of_a_radial_link.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Finite_subdivision_of_a_radial_link.png/500px-Finite_subdivision_of_a_radial_link.png 2x" data-file-width="541" data-file-height="546" /></a><figcaption>A fractal generated by a <a href="/wiki/Finite_subdivision_rule" title="Finite subdivision rule">finite subdivision rule</a> for an <a href="/wiki/Alternating_link" class="mw-redirect" title="Alternating link">alternating link</a></figcaption></figure>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Finite_subdivision_rule" title="Finite subdivision rule">Finite subdivision rules</a></i> – use a recursive <a href="/wiki/Topological" class="mw-redirect" title="Topological">topological</a> algorithm for refining tilings<sup id="cite&#95;ref-finite&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finite-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and they are similar to the process of <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-biol&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biol-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The iterative processes used in creating the <a href="/wiki/Cantor_set" title="Cantor set">Cantor set</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sierpinski_carpet" class="mw-redirect" title="Sierpinski carpet">Sierpinski carpet</a> are examples of finite subdivision rules, as is <a href="/wiki/Barycentric_subdivision" title="Barycentric subdivision">barycentric subdivision</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Applications">Applications</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Simulated_fractals">Simulated fractals</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Simulated fractals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Julia-Set_z2%2Bc_ani.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Julia-Set_z2%2Bc_ani.gif/250px-Julia-Set_z2%2Bc_ani.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Julia-Set_z2%2Bc_ani.gif/400px-Julia-Set_z2%2Bc_ani.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Julia-Set_z2%2Bc_ani.gif 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="300" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Fractal_art" title="Fractal art">Fractal art</a> made from a <a href="/wiki/Julia_set" title="Julia set">Julia-Set</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Fractal patterns have been modeled extensively, albeit within a range of scales rather than infinitely, owing to the practical limits of physical time and space. Models may simulate theoretical fractals or <a href="#fractals_in_nature">natural phenomena with fractal features</a>. The outputs of the modelling process may be highly artistic renderings, outputs for investigation, or benchmarks for <a href="/wiki/Fractal_analysis" title="Fractal analysis">fractal analysis</a>. Some specific applications of fractals to technology are listed <a href="#fractals_in_technology">elsewhere</a>. Images and other outputs of modelling are normally referred to as being "fractals" even if they do not have strictly fractal characteristics, such as when it is possible to zoom into a region of the fractal image that does not exhibit any fractal properties. Also, these may include calculation or display <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(error)" title="Artifact (error)">artifacts</a> which are not characteristics of true fractals.
</p><p>Modeled fractals may be sounds,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-music&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-music-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> digital images, electrochemical patterns, <a href="/wiki/Circadian_rhythm" title="Circadian rhythm">circadian rhythms</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> etc.
Fractal patterns have been reconstructed in physical 3-dimensional space<sup id="cite&#95;ref-medicine&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medicine-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10">&#58;&#8202;10&#8202;</span></sup> and virtually, often called "<a href="/wiki/In_silico" title="In silico">in silico</a>" modeling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-modeling&#95;vasculature&#95;46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modeling_vasculature-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Models of fractals are generally created using <a href="/wiki/Fractal-generating_software" title="Fractal-generating software">fractal-generating software</a> that implements techniques such as those outlined above.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-vicsek&#95;4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vicsek-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-time&#95;series&#95;13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_series-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-medicine&#95;21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medicine-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As one illustration, trees, ferns, cells of the nervous system,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-branching&#95;18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-branching-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> blood and lung vasculature,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-modeling&#95;vasculature&#95;46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modeling_vasculature-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and other branching <a href="/wiki/Patterns_in_nature" title="Patterns in nature">patterns in nature</a> can be modeled on a computer by using recursive <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> and <a href="/wiki/L-systems" class="mw-redirect" title="L-systems">L-systems</a> techniques.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-branching&#95;18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-branching-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The recursive nature of some patterns is obvious in certain examples—a branch from a tree or a <a href="/wiki/Frond" title="Frond">frond</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Fern" title="Fern">fern</a> is a miniature replica of the whole: not identical, but similar in nature. Similarly, random fractals have been used to describe/create many highly irregular real-world objects, such as coastlines and mountains. A limitation of modeling fractals is that resemblance of a fractal model to a natural phenomenon does not prove that the phenomenon being modeled is formed by a process similar to the modeling algorithms.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="fractals&#95;in&#95;nature"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Natural_phenomena_with_fractal_features">Natural phenomena with fractal features</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fractal&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Natural phenomena with fractal features"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Patterns_in_nature" title="Patterns in nature">Patterns in nature</a></div>
<p>Approximate fractals found in nature display self-similarity over extended, but finite, scale ranges. The connection between fractals and leaves, for instance, is currently being used to determine how much carbon is contained in trees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Phenomena known to have fractal features include: 
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1184024115">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 20em;">

<li><a href="/wiki/Animal_coloration" title="Animal coloration">Animal coloration</a> patterns</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">Blood vessels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_vessels" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulmonary vessels">pulmonary vessels</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-modeling&#95;vasculature&#95;46-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modeling_vasculature-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>

<li>Clouds and rainfall areas<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>

<li>Heart rates<sup id="cite&#95;ref-heart&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heart-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>

		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 156px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 154px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Lichtenberg_figure_in_block_of_Plexiglas.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="High-voltage breakdown within a 4&#160;in (100&#160;mm) block of acrylic glass creates a fractal Lichtenberg figure"><img alt="High-voltage breakdown within a 4&#160;in (100&#160;mm) block of acrylic glass creates a fractal Lichtenberg figure" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Lichtenberg_figure_in_block_of_Plexiglas.jpg/250px-Lichtenberg_figure_in_block_of_Plexiglas.jpg" decoding="async" width="154" height="140" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Lichtenberg_figure_in_block_of_Plexiglas.jpg/500px-Lichtenberg_figure_in_block_of_Plexiglas.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="726" /></a></span></div>
